Ladies and Gentlemen: 
This report presents the data collected and results of the drilled shaft load test 
program performed at the location designated as Load Test Shaft LT-B in Waipahu for 
the Honolulu High-Capacity Transit Corridor Project (HHCTCP) in the District of 
Waipahu on the Island of Oahu, Hawaii. 
Please note that the soil samples recovered during our field exploration 
(remaining after testing) will be stored for a period of two months from the date of this 
report. The samples will be discarded after that date unless arrangements are made for 
a longer storage period. Please contact our office for alternative sample storage 
requirements, if appropriate. 
If you have questions or need additional information, please contact our office. 
Very truly yours,

SECTION 1.  OBJECTIVES 
The Honolulu High-Capacity Transit Corridor Project (HHCTCP) involves the 
construction and operation of a fixed-guideway transit system in the corridor between 
Kapolei and the University of Hawaii at Manoa with a branch to Waikiki on the Island of 
Oahu, Hawaii. This report presents the results of the drilled shaft load test program 
performed at a load test shaft, designated as LT-B, adjacent to a portion of the 
fixed-guideway alignment in the Waipahu area on the Island of Oahu, Hawaii. The 
project location and general vicinity are shown on the Project Location Map, Plate 1. 
The proposed high-capacity transit system will have an elevated fixed-guideway 
system with a typical 150-foot long span between bent columns. Relatively large axial 
loads and high overturning moments typically are generated by these long span structures 
with large live loads. We understand non-redundant drilled shaft foundations (single drilled 
shaft supporting each bent column) have been considered for support of the elevated 
guideway structure planned for the project to reduce the foundation footprint and to reduce 
the need for extensive relocation of the existing underground utilities. 
In general, drilled shaft foundations would derive vertical support from a 
combination of skin friction between the drilled shaft and the surrounding subsurface 
materials and from end bearing at the base of the drilled shaft. The drilled shaft 
foundations would derive a large portion of the bearing support from adhesion between 
the sides of the drilled shaft and the surrounding soils and/or rock formation. Therefore, 
the adhesion values and end-bearing contribution used in the analyses would have a 
significant impact on the lengths of the drilled shaft foundations planned for the project 
design. 
In order to provide additional information and data for the drilled shaft foundation 
design, we have undertaken a drilled shaft load test program, including the performance 
of an instrumented load test, to fulfill the following objectives: 
To evaluate the adhesion and end-bearing values of the drilled shaft 
embedded in the recent alluvium and older alluvium deposits at the load 
test site.

To observe the methods and equipment used to install the high-capacity

To observe the method of concrete placement by the load test shaft

the drilled shaft.

SECTION 2.  SCOPE OF WORK 
The purpose of our drilled shaft load test program was to evaluate the adhesion 
and end-bearing values of the recent alluvium and older alluvium deposits anticipated in 
the Waipahu area of the project alignment. Our work was performed in general 
accordance with the Agreement for Professional Services entered into on June 30, 2009 
with the City and County of Honolulu, Department of Transportation Services. In order 
to accomplish this, we conducted a drilled shaft load test program at the LT-B location 
consisting of the following tasks and efforts: 
1. 
Research and review of available in-house geologic and soils information 
in the project vicinity.  
2. 
Coordination of utility toning and clearance of the boring and load test 
shaft locations with various utility companies. 
3. 
Mobilization and demobilization of a truck-mounted drill rig and operators 
to and from the project site. 
4. 
Drilling and sampling of one (1) boring extending to a depth of about 
163.5 feet below the existing ground surface. 
5. 
Backfilling of the drilled boring with bentonite pellets. 
6. 
Coordination of the field exploration and logging of the boring by our 
geologist. 
7. 
Laboratory testing of selected soil samples retrieved from our field 
exploration as an aid in classifying the materials and evaluating their 
engineering properties. 
8. 
Excavation of an 8-foot nominal diameter load test shaft by our 
subcontractor, CMZ of Hawaii, Inc., extending to a depth of approximately 
125.2 feet below the existing ground surface. 
9. 
Performance of a drilled shaft base inspection utilizing a Miniature Shaft 
Inspection Device (Mini-SID) to check the base of the drilled shaft for 
cleanliness when using the end-bearing component for foundation 
support. 
10. 
Assembly of the crosshole sonic logging test tubes onto the upper portion 
of the reinforcing steel cage (above the load cell) by CMZ Hawaii, Inc. 
11. 
Assembly of the load test instrumentation, including the steel plates, 
Osterberg load cell, strain gages, etc., onto the reinforcing steel cage by

SECTION 3.  SITE CHARACTERIZATION 
3.1 
Regional Geology 
The Island of Oahu generally is composed of the weathered and dissected 
remnants of two extinct shield (domed) volcanoes: Waianae Volcano and Koolau 
Volcano. The older Waianae Volcano forms the bulk of the western third of Oahu’s 
landmass while the younger Koolau Volcano forms a major portion of the eastern 
two-thirds of Oahu’s landmass. Generally, it is believed that the Waianae and Koolau 
Shield Volcanoes began erupting on the order of about 20 to 25 million years ago as 
deep ocean submarine volcanoes. Subaerial volcanic eruptions on the Island of Oahu 
ended approximately 2.2 to 3.5 million years ago in the Pleistocene Epoch. Furthermore, 
the Waianae Volcano became extinct while the Koolau Volcano remained active, 
therefore, the eastern flank of Waianae Volcano is partially overlapped and buried below 
the younger Koolau Volcano lavas near the central interior portions of the Island of Oahu. 
In addition to the primary volcanic composition of the Island of Oahu, other 
secondary and generally more localized geologic earth materials comprise the lowland, 
coastal, and valley portions of the island. The secondary earth materials include various 
alluvial and colluvial deposits, oceanic and marine sediments, submerged and emerged 
coralline reef materials, and surface fills. 
The subsurface geological conditions at the load test shaft location (LT-B) are 
typified by various terrigenous alluvial deposits with embedded cobbles and boulders, 
buried volcanic materials consisting of residual and saprolitic soils and weathered 
basaltic rock formation at greater depth. The geomorphology and subsurface conditions 
at the site are directly related to the glacio-eustatic fluctuations of the sea level during 
the Pleistocene Epoch and the genesis of Pearl Harbor and the Honolulu Coastal Plain. 
The subsurface conditions at the project site are dominated by fossil Pleistocene age 
alluvial soil deposits and related deposits laid during high stands of the sea that are 
intercalated with terrigenous sediments. The base of the stratigraphic section in this 
area is Koolau Basalt. Evidence from wells drilled indicates that the Island of Oahu has 
subsided by as much as 1,200 feet since the cessation of this early volcanic activity 
(Macdonald and Abbott, 1970). 
LT-B  11/20/2009
SECTION 3.  SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6000-40 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 6 
Hawaii ● California 
During the Pleistocene Epoch (most recent Ice Age), many sea-level fluctuations 
occurred because of the waxing and waning of global continental glaciation. Generally 
speaking, as the great continental glaciers accumulated, the global level of the oceans 
fell because there was less water available to fill the oceanic basins. Conversely, as the 
glaciers receded (melted), the global level of the oceans rose because of the water 
influx. The landmass elevation of the Island of Oahu remained essentially stable during 
these sea-level changes; therefore, the fluctuations were eustatic in nature. The 
glacio-eustatic fluctuations resulted in stands of the sea that were either higher or lower 
relative to the present sea-level conditions. The following are some generalized 
examples of the geomorphic effects that resulted from the changes in sea level relative 
to the island landmass. 
The higher sea-level stands caused the accumulation of deltas and depositional 
fans composed of terrigenous sediments at the heads of old bays. Furthermore, the 
higher sea stands caused accumulation of coralline reef materials at correspondingly 
higher elevations and the deposition of lagoonal and marine sediments in the quiet 
backwaters protected from wave action by fringing coral reefs. 
The lower sea-level stands caused streams to incise erosional valleys into the 
existing sediments and reef platforms. In addition, the subaerial exposure of the marine 
sediments and calcareous materials caused consolidation of the soft deltaic materials 
and/or lagoonal deposits and hardening of the calcareous reef materials. Finally, 
renewed subaerial erosion (acting on the upper regions of the volcanic dome) caused 
the mobilization and deposition of terrigenous alluvial soils within relatively high energy 
depositional environments, which is typified by a poorly sorted mix of alluvial materials 
with a greater range in particle sizes. During periods of no significant sea level change, 
continued stream flow extended the alluvial deltas and fans seaward and allowed the 
deposition of alluvium overlying pre-existing lagoonal sediments. 
Development projects at the load test shaft location have resulted in alterations 
to the natural terrain including the placement of earth fills, which may be of poor quality 
in terms of supporting large structural loads.

3.2 
Subsurface Characteristics 
In general, we observed three primary stratigraphic units at the load test shaft 
location (LT-B). The following sections briefly describe the general characteristics of the 
three stratigraphic units based on literature research, past experience with similar 
materials, and the field exploration and laboratory testing program conducted at the load 
test shaft location. 
3.2.1 Older Alluvium 
Older alluvial deposits are generally very stiff to hard silty and clayey soils that 
originally were deposited in alluvial environments, buried over long periods of 
time by more recent deposits to become somewhat consolidated, and then 
subsequently uncovered at the ground surface by erosion or other geologic 
processes. This unit generally overlies the Koolau Basalt and consists of 
terrigenous sediments, which have been transported by stream action from the 
upper reaches of the streams.  
Gravel lenses representing buried stream channels sometimes are encountered. 
Portions of the Older Alluvium contain weathered basaltic cobbles and boulders. 
These coarse-grained portions of the unit also may contain artesian groundwater 
from the basal fresh water system or as a sub-aquifer. The deposits may 
resemble saprolite soil deposits but are clearly differentiable by the layered 
characteristics. 
Older Alluvium materials generally exhibit high strength, which leads to a 
relatively high bearing or load carrying capacity with generally low settlement 
characteristics. Frequently, these materials show moderate to high shrink/swell 
potential when subjected to fluctuations in the moisture contents. However, this is 
not considered a constraint for the type of structural systems proposed for this 
project. Structurally, the Older Alluvium materials are considered to be sufficiently 
competent to support relatively large structural loads with low settlement 
characteristics when designed properly. 
LT-B  11/20/2009
SECTION 3.  SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6000-40 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 8 
Hawaii ● California 
3.2.2 Recent Alluvium 
These soils are generally the result of sedimentation in a very low energy 
environment such as an estuary or bay. Frequently, the soils assigned to this unit 
accumulated very rapidly under water and might not have been exposed to air. 
Therefore, there is a tendency to encounter under-consolidated sediments, which 
settle under the weight of minimal overburden or structural loading. Recent 
alluvium generally is found within some stream channels and bordering some 
stream banks, at the mouths (outlets) of streams, and within broad valley floors 
and basin features surrounded by hills. These soils also may be found in 
proximity to swamp and tidal flat environments. 
Recent alluvial soils generally are considered poorly to very poorly suited for use 
as support for foundation loads, due to their low in-situ strength characteristics 
and the tendency for excessive settlement to occur in structures overlying soils of 
this unit. The Recent Alluvium unit also includes the following subunits:  Recent 
Alluvium; Lagoonal Deposits; and Marsh Deposits. 
The Recent Alluvium subunit generally consists of tan to brown silty clays with 
varying amounts of sands and infrequent pockets of gravel and cobbles. The 
consistency is generally in the range of very soft to medium stiff. 
The Lagoonal Deposit subunit generally consists of light gray to medium gray 
sandy silts or silty sands in a very soft to soft consistency and/or very loose to loose 
relative density. Gravel sized fragments are rare. Frequently, the silts are organic in 
origin and may have a mild to strong organic odor.  
The Marsh Deposit subunit generally consists of dark gray and brown silts and 
clays with fibrous organic materials in a very soft to soft consistency. This soil unit 
contains traces of fine to medium grained sands. They are characterized by a 
moderate to strong organic odor and may contain small fragile mollusk shells. 
3.2.3 Fill 
The majority of the low lying areas is underlain at relatively shallow depths by 
recent alluvium and was originally marsh areas that have been filled during 
LT-B  11/20/2009
SECTION 3.  SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6000-40 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 9 
Hawaii ● California 
urbanization. The area where LT-B is situated had been filled over lagoonal 
deposits and/or marsh deposits underlain by older alluvial soil deposits. In 
general, the fill materials consist of clayey silts and sandy silts with stiff to hard 
consistencies. The fills were intended only to support very light structures on 
shallow spread footings and, therefore, only form a thin crust over the soft recent 
alluvium. 
The quality and composition of the fill materials are variable. These fills were 
frequently placed without proper compaction control. Therefore, it is difficult to 
make any general statements regarding their engineering properties or 
construction in these areas. 
3.2.4 Groundwater 
The load test shaft location (LT-B) is within the Southern Oahu regional aquifer 
system, which is the largest basal groundwater area on the Island of Oahu. Koolau 
Basalt is the primary aquifer in the central and eastern parts of the area, whereas 
the Waianae Volcanics forms the main aquifer in the western part. Unconsolidated 
and consolidated sedimentary deposits form a thick confining unit (“caprock”) near 
the coast. 
Water levels in the Southern Oahu groundwater area generally range from about 
25 to 30 feet above sea level inland to about 15 to 20 feet above sea level near the 
shore where the water is under artesian pressure because it is confined by caprock. 
The caprock impedes the seaward movement of fresh groundwater. In the eastern 
part of the area, thick valley fill and underlying weathered rocks form partial barriers 
to groundwater flow. In the western part of the area, the weathered zone near the 
unconformity separating Koolau Basalt from the underlying Waianae Volcanics 
impedes the flow of water between the two volcanic rock aquifers. 
We encountered groundwater in the drilled boring at a depth of about 11 feet below 
the existing ground surface at the time of our field exploration. The groundwater 
level measured during our field exploration generally corresponded to about 
Elevation -3 feet Mean Sea Level (MSL). It should be noted that groundwater levels 
LT-B  11/20/2009

could fluctuate depending on seasonal precipitation, groundwater withdrawal and/or 
injection, and other factors. 
In addition, we wish to point out that we encountered a slight artesian 
groundwater condition in the boring drilled. When the groundwater level was 
allowed to stabilize overnight, the groundwater level rose to about 2 to 3 feet 
above the existing ground surface elevation. This condition is characteristic of the 
previous borings drilled in the Waipahu area of the alignment. 
3.3 
Subsurface Conditions 
The subsurface conditions at the load test shaft location (LT-B) discussed herein 
were explored by drilling and sampling one boring, designated as Boring No. 2 (B-2), at 
the approximate location shown on the Site Plan, Plate 2. Boring No. 2 was drilled 
adjacent to the load test shaft location (LT-B). The ground surface elevation at the 
boring location is at about +8 feet MSL based on the available topographic survey 
information. Generalized discussions of the subsurface soil and groundwater conditions 
at the load test shaft location (LT-B) are presented below. 
Subsurface data (from B-2) indicate that the existing ground at the load test site 
generally is underlain by a clayey fill layer extending to about 6 feet deep overlying soft 
lagoonal deposits. The lagoonal deposits consisted of gray silty clays and clayey silts of 
very soft to soft consistency. The lagoonal deposits graded to a stiff consistency at 
about 50 feet in depth. The lagoonal deposit overlies older alluvium consisting of very 
stiff to hard clayey soils with some sand, gravel, and cobbles. The older alluvium graded 
to a coarse-grained granular layer consisting of highly weathered gravel and cobbles in 
a clayey soil matrix at about 92 feet below the ground surface. The older alluvial 
deposits extended to the maximum depth of the boring at about 163.5 feet below the 
existing ground surface. 
Groundwater was encountered at about 11.2 feet below the ground surface in the 
boring drilled at the time of our field exploration. The groundwater level observed during 
drilling corresponded to about Elevation -3 feet MSL. Due to the low permeability nature 
of the subsurface soils and the artesian groundwater conditions, this water level

SECTION 4.  DRILLED SHAFT LOAD TEST PROGRAM 
The drilled shaft load test program conducted at the load test shaft LT-B location 
of the HHCTCP generally consisted of the following field and laboratory work 
components. A brief description of each work component is presented below. Detailed 
discussions, findings, and the test results of the various components are provided in the 
respective appendices herein. 
4.1 
Soil Borings and Associated Field Testing 
Soil sampling methods and field testing are described in more detail in the 
introduction to Appendix A. The following discussion provides an overview of the 
methods and procedures utilized in the test boring exploration. 
The soil boring was advanced with 4-inch diameter continuous-flight solid-stem 
augers and PQ wireline coring methods. Sampling of the soils was performed using the 
modified California sampler (ASTM D 3550), the Standard Penetration Test (SPT) split 
spoon sampler (ASTM D 1586), and thin-walled Shelby Tubes (ASTM D 1587). 
In-situ testing of the subsurface materials in the boring included the SPT, which 
was performed in accordance with ASTM D 1586. SPT sampling provides a measure of 
the resistance or consistency of a soil and is reported as the uncorrected N-value, which 
is defined as the number of blows required to drive a standard split-spoon sampler 
12 inches with a 140-lb hammer, after 6 inches of initial seating. The penetration 
resistance of the modified California sampler was recorded in a similar manner, and the 
modified California sampler blows per 12 inches can be approximately related to the 
SPT N-value through correlation. The thin-walled Shelby tubes were hydraulically 
pushed into the soil formation, and the hydraulic pressures needed to advance the tube 
per foot are recorded on the logs of borings. 
During the field exploration program, our geologist performed pocket 
penetrometer tests on selected cohesive soil samples retrieved. Pocket penetrometer 
tests provide an indication of the unconfined compressive strength of a soil sample. The 
pocket penetrometer test values are shown on the Logs of Borings in Appendix A.

4.2 
Laboratory Tests 
Laboratory tests were performed on selected soil samples to determine the 
physical and engineering properties. Laboratory tests performed included natural 
moisture content, in-situ unit weight, Atterberg limits, grain size distribution, 
consolidation, and unconsolidated-undrained (UU) triaxial compression. The laboratory 
test results are summarized on the log of boring, where appropriate, and graphic 
presentation of the test results are provided in Appendix B. 
4.3 
Load Test Shaft Installation 
An 8-foot nominal diameter drilled shaft (designated as LT-B) was excavated to a 
depth of approximately 125.2 feet below the existing ground surface at the approximate 
location shown on the Site Plan, Plate 2. The load test shaft location (LT-B) is at a site off 
the HHCTCP fixed-guideway alignment. Load Test Shaft LT-B is about 500 feet to the 
north of Sta. 662+00 (approximately) of the alignment as shown on the Site Plan (Plate 2). 
The ground surface elevation at the load test shaft location (LT-B) is at approximately 
+8 feet MSL. 
The load test shaft was structurally reinforced and instrumented with thirteen (13) 
levels of embedment strain gages for load testing purposes, as shown on the Drilled Shaft 
Load Test (LT-B) Detail, Plate 3. As a minimum, embedment strain gages were provided 
at each level with additional strain gages at selected levels where we believe would 
provide for some additional information. In addition, some thermistors also were installed 
to supplement the concrete temperature measurements in the drilled shaft concrete from 
the embedment strain gages for mass concrete considerations. 
During the load test shaft installation for LT-B, a Geolabs engineer observed the 
materials encountered during the shaft excavation, and a log of the subsurface materials 
encountered was maintained. In addition, the Geolabs engineer also maintained a log of 
the concrete volume placed in the load test shaft as a function of depth throughout the 
concrete placement. The log maintained by the Geolabs engineer is presented in 
Appendix C. 
LT-B  11/20/2009
SECTION 4.  DRILLED SHAFT LOAD TEST PROGRAM  
W.O. 6000-40 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 14 
Hawaii ● California 
There were some anomalies encountered during the concrete placement for the 
load test shaft. During the initial placement of the concrete, the concrete pumping 
operation shook appreciably up until the fourth truck load of concrete. During placement of 
the fourth truck load of concrete (and subsequently), the pumping operation was much 
smoother. It was later discovered that the last segment of the tremie pipe disengaged at 
the bottom joint (32-foot segment), and concrete likely was discharging at the broken joint 
above the top of the concrete level in the test shaft hole instead of discharging at the 
bottom of the tremie pipe. Concrete placement for the load test shaft was halted 
temporarily at that point to remove the tremie pipe.  
Several attempts were made to retrieve the lower segment of the tremie pipe from 
within the load test shaft concrete without success. There was too much concrete placed 
in the test shaft hole to remove the reinforcing cage and the concrete placed. Removal of 
the reinforcing cage likely would have damaged the instrumentation and the load cell. 
Therefore, the decision was made to continue the concrete placement even with the 
potentially poor quality concrete in the load test shaft.  
The poor quality concrete in the load test shaft was later confirmed by the 
Crosshole Sonic Logging (CSL) tests and from the load testing. A replacement load test 
shaft was proposed to replace the LT-B with poor quality concrete; however, the 
replacement load test shaft for LT-B was not executed due to time constraints (expiring 
Right-of-Entry document) and other considerations. 
4.4 
Miniature Shaft Inspection Device 
To engage the end bearing component of the drilled shaft, the base of the drilled 
shaft excavation will need to be relatively clean and free of loose sediments before 
concrete placement. To check the base of the drilled shaft before concrete placement 
and to provide a higher degree of confidence that the drilled shaft base is relatively 
clean and free of appreciable loose sediments, we utilized the miniature Shaft 
Inspection Device (Mini-SID) to check the base of the drilled shaft for cleanliness when 
using the end-bearing component for foundation support.  
LT-B  11/20/2009
SECTION 4.  DRILLED SHAFT LOAD TEST PROGRAM  
W.O. 6000-40 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 15 
Hawaii ● California 
The Mini-SID was manufactured by GPE, Inc. of Gainesville, Florida. The device 
consists of a waterproof encased color video camera mounted in an inspection bell. The 
inspection bell is lowered to the bottom of the drilled shaft with the aid of an electrical 
motor hoist. Once the inspection bell is seated on the bottom of the drilled shaft, live 
video is transmitted to a video control unit where the Engineer, Inspector, and 
Contractor can view and evaluate the shaft bottom at the same time. The drilled shaft is 
considered acceptable for end-bearing support if 50% or more of the shaft bottom 
contains 0.5 inches or less of sediments. If any part of the shaft bottom contains more 
than 1.5 inches of sediments, the drilled shaft is considered unacceptable. The 
methodology of the visual inspection and the results from the Mini-SID performed on 
Load Test Shaft LT-B are presented in Appendix D. 
The results of the Mini-SID performed on LT-B generally show that the average 
for all five views of the shaft bottom containing less than 0.5 inches of sediment is 40%, 
which is less than the typical 50% threshold. After several attempts to clean the shaft 
bottom with no significant improvement to reduce the relatively small amount of 
sediments observed at the base of the load test shaft, we recommended proceeding 
with the reinforcing cage installation and concrete placement even with only an average 
of 40% of the shaft bottom with less than 0.5 inches of sediments because of the nature 
of the bearing materials (cobbles and gravel in a fine grained soil matrix). It should be 
noted that none of the five views had an unacceptable amount of sediments (greater 
than 1.5 inches of sediments). 
4.5 
Concrete Tests 
Temperature control of mass concrete is necessary to prevent cracking caused 
by excessive tensile strains that result from differential cooling of the concrete. The 
concrete is heated by reaction of cement with water and can gain additional heat from 
exposure to the ambient conditions. Cracking may be controlled by methods that limit 
the peak temperature to a safe level, so the tensile strains developed as the concrete 
cools to equilibrium are less than the tensile strain capacity. For the HHCTCP project, 
we understand the maximum temperature of the concrete has been specified at 
160 degrees Fahrenheit or less and the maximum differential between the center and 
the edge of the concrete mass is specified at 35 degrees Fahrenheit or less. 
LT-B  11/20/2009
SECTION 4.  DRILLED SHAFT LOAD TEST PROGRAM  
W.O. 6000-40 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 16 
Hawaii ● California 
Temperature measurements of the concrete placed in the Load Test Shaft LT-B 
were recorded for about 9 days since concrete placement, and the measured 
temperatures are presented in Appendix E. In addition, slump tests on fresh concrete 
and compression tests on the concrete cylinders were performed, and the test results 
also are summarized in Appendix E. 
Some erratic readings were noted as evident in some of the temperature plots. 
The erratic readings in the temperature plots likely were due to water in the concrete 
migrating into the multiplexer housing through the strain gage and thermistor wires 
causing corrosion of the multiplexer connections. This was apparent after about 5 to 
6 days of monitoring (please refer to Plates E-11 through E-15). The multiplexer 
connections were corroded beyond repair. Please also disregard the temperature plot 
on Plate E-5 (Level 3 at 101.5-foot level) because this was apparently the area 
containing the poor quality concrete from the detached tremie pipe. 
4.6 
Crosshole Sonic Logging 
Crosshole Sonic Logging (CSL) testing was performed to evaluate the integrity of 
the concrete drilled shaft. The CSL testing was conducted using the CHUM CSL Testing 
equipment manufactured by Piletest. CSL tests were performed between all the 
possible access tube combinations in the load test shaft (8 tubes in the load test shaft 
resulting in 28 profiles). 
Steel access tubes were installed in the shafts. Generally, the steel access tubes 
were extended approximately 7 feet above the top of the shaft concrete for access 
considerations. Potable water was used to fill the access tubes during and following 
concrete placement, and the Contractor monitored the water level in the access tubes to 
keep the water level in the access tubes until the CSL testing. The methodology and 
results of the CSL tests are presented in Appendix E. 
4.7 
Osterberg Load Test 
Due to the high capacities anticipated for the drilled shafts, conventional load tests 
would not be practical and would be costly to conduct. Therefore, bi-directional axial load 
test using expandable load cells (Osterberg Load Cells) was adopted for the drilled shaft 
LT-B  11/20/2009
SECTION 4.  DRILLED SHAFT LOAD TEST PROGRAM  
W.O. 6000-40 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 17 
Hawaii ● California 
load test program. The bi-directional load test separately tests the shear resistance and 
end-bearing components of the drilled shaft by loading the shaft in two directions (upward 
for shear resistance, and downward for end-bearing and shear resistance). 
The Osterberg Load Cells was capable of applying a load of at least 6,000 kips in 
each direction. The expandable load cell was attached to the reinforcing steel cage prior 
to lowering the cage into the drilled hole. The drilled shaft load test was performed in 
general accordance with the Quick Load Test Method of ASTM D 1143. The load test 
shaft was loaded to failure to evaluate the ultimate side shear resistance and 
end-bearing components of the shaft. Installation of the expandable load cells, 
installation of the embedment strain gages, performance of the bi-directional axial load 
tests, and presentation of the load test data were performed by LOADTEST, Inc. 
The maximum sustained bi-directional load applied to the load test shaft was 
2,886 kips. At the maximum test load, the displacements above and below the O-cell 
were 0.795 inches and 0.109 inches, respectively. Significant deformations of the upper 
bearing plate were observed during the load testing, and crushing of the concrete was 
inferred from the top bearing plate telltale measurements. Extremely high, non-uniform 
strain readings from the first two levels of strain gages above the O-cell also suggest a 
very low-modulus concrete above the load cell. This likely has resulted from a problem 
during construction, wherein an unknown amount of concrete was deposited through 
water due to a disengaged tremie pipe. 
The load test data and a discussion on the load test results are presented in the 
report prepared by LOADTEST, Inc. in Appendix G. 
END OF DRILLED SHAFT LOAD TEST PROGRAM 
LT-B  11/20/2009
W.O. 6000-40 
GEOLABS, INC.

Hawaii ● California 
SECTION 5.  LIMITATIONS 
The geotechnical field and laboratory test data submitted in this report are based 
upon information obtained from the field exploration point. Variations of subsurface 
conditions beyond the exploration point may occur, and the nature and extent of these 
variations may not become evident until additional field investigation and/or construction 
is underway. 
The location of the field exploration point (B-2) indicated in this report have been 
estimated based on field measurements from visible features shown on the Site Plan. 
The elevation of the boring was interpolated between the spot elevations and contour 
lines shown on the topographic survey map. The physical locations and elevations of 
the field exploration points should be considered accurate only to the degree implied by 
the methods used. 
The stratification lines shown on the graphic representations of the field 
exploration points depict the approximate boundaries between soil and rock types and, 
as such, may denote a gradual transition. Water level data presented on the log of 
boring in this report were based on measurements recorded in the boring at the times 
shown on the graphic representations. It should be noted that fluctuation of the water 
level may occur due to variation in tides, rainfall, temperature, and other factors. 
This report has been prepared for the exclusive use of the City and County of 
Honolulu, Department of Transportation Services, for specific application to the 
Honolulu High-Capacity Transit Corridor Project in accordance with generally accepted 
geotechnical engineering principles and practices. No warranty is expressed or implied. 
This report is limited in scope and is for the project design advancement and cost 
estimation purposes only. The boring for this drilled shaft load test program did not 
encounter cavities or underground structures, such as obstructions, buried debris, 
storage tanks, etc., that may be present at the project site.  
This report was not intended to investigate the potential for presence of 
hazardous materials existing at the project site. It should be noted that the equipment,

techniques, and personnel used to conduct a geo-environmental exploration differ 
substantially from those applied in geotechnical engineering.

A P P E N D I X   A 
Field Exploration 
We explored the subsurface conditions at the location of the drilled shaft load test 
(designated as LT-B) by drilling and sampling one boring, designated as Boring No. 2. The 
boring extended to a depth of approximately 163.5 feet below the existing ground surface 
using a Mobile B-80 truck-mounted drill rig equipped with continuous-flight augers and 
coring tools. The field exploration was conducted on July 25, July 27 thru July 29, 2009. 
The approximate boring location is shown on the Site Plan, Plate 2. 
Generally, the soil boring was advanced with 4-inch diameter continuous-flight 
solid-stem augers and PQ coring methods. After the boring was advanced to the 
maximum depth of the borehole, the bored hole generally was backfilled with bentonite 
pellets starting from the bottom of the hole. 
Our geologist classified the materials encountered in the boring by visual and 
textural examination in the field in general accordance with ASTM D 2488, Standard 
Practice for Description and Identification of Soils, and monitored the drilling operations on 
a near-continuous basis. These classifications were further reviewed visually and by 
testing in the laboratory. Soils were classified in general accordance with ASTM D 2487, 
Standard Practice for Classification of Soils for Engineering Purposes (Unified Soil 
Classification System), as shown on the Soil Log Legend (Plate A-0.1). Graphic 
representations of the materials encountered in the boring are presented on the Log of 
Boring, Plate A-1. 
Relatively “undisturbed” soil samples were obtained from the drilled boring in 
general accordance with ASTM D 3550, Standard Practice for Thick Wall, Ring-Lined, 
Spilt Barrel, Drive Sampling of Soils, by driving a 3-inch OD modified California sampler 
with a 140-pound hammer falling 30 inches. In addition, some samples were obtained from 
the drilled boring in general accordance with ASTM D 1586, Standard Test Method for 
Penetration Test and Split-Barrel Sampling of Soils, by driving a 2-inch OD standard 
penetration sampler using the same hammer and drop. The blow counts needed to drive 
the sampler the second and third 6 inches of an 18-inch drive are shown as the 
“Penetration Resistance” on the Logs of Borings at the appropriate sample depths.  
Relatively “undisturbed” samples of the soft soil formation were obtained by pushing a 
3-inch OD thin-walled stainless steel Shelby tube into the soil formation in general 
accordance with ASTM D 1587, Standard Practice for Thin-Walled Tube Sampling of Soils 
for Geotechnical Purposes. 
Pocket penetrometer tests were performed on selected cohesive soil samples 
retrieved in the field. The pocket penetrometer test provides an indication of the 
unconfined compressive strength of the soil sample. Pocket penetrometer test results are 
presented on the Log of Boring at the appropriate sample depths. 
LT-B  11/20/2009
Appendix A 
Field Exploration 
W.O. 6000-40 
GEOLABS, INC. 
NOVEMBER 2009     Page A-2 
Hawaii ● California 
Core samples of the rock materials encountered at the project site were obtained 
by using diamond core drilling techniques, consisting of a PQ wireline coring system with 
double tube core barrel (core size of 3.35 inches), in general accordance with 
ASTM D 2113, Diamond Core Drilling for Site Investigation. Core drilling is a rotary drilling 
method that uses a hollow bit to cut into the rock formation. The rock material left in the 
hollow core of the bit is mechanically recovered for examination and description. Rock 
cores were described in general accordance with the Rock Description System, as shown 
on the Rock Log Legend, Plate A-0.2. The Rock Description System is based on the 
publication “Suggested Methods for the Quantitative Description of Discontinuities in Rock 
Masses” by International Society for Rock Mechanics (March 1977).  
Recovery (REC) may be used as a subjective guide to the interpretation of the 
relative quality of rock masses, where appropriate. Recovery is defined as the actual 
length of material recovered from a coring attempt versus the length of the core attempt. 
For example, if 3.7 feet of material is recovered from a 5.0-foot core run, the recovery 
would be 74 percent and would be shown on the Logs of Borings as REC = 74%. 
The Rock Quality Designation (RQD) is also a subjective guide to the relative 
quality of rock masses. RQD is defined as the percentage of the core run that is sound 
material in excess of 4 inches in length without any discontinuities, discounting any drilling, 
mechanical, and handling induced fractures or breaks. If 2.5 feet of sound material is 
recovered from a 5.0-foot core run, the RQD would be 50 percent and would be shown on 
the Logs of Borings as RQD = 50%. Generally, the following is used to describe the 
relative quality of the rock based on the "Practical Handbook of Physical Properties of 
Rocks and Minerals” by Robert S. Carmichael (1989). 
Rock Quality 
RQD 
(%) 
Very Poor 
0 – 25 
Poor 
25 – 50 
Fair 
50 – 75 
Good 
75 – 90 
Excellent 
90 – 100 
It should be noted that we did not encounter rock formation in the boring drilled; 
however, we did encounter highly weathered cobbles and boulder materials embedded in 
the older alluvium within the boring. Therefore, PQ coring methods were used to advance 
the boring to the full depth of the borehole. Photographs of the recovered materials from 
the core runs are presented on Plate A-2 for reference.

Specimen breaks with difficulty after several "pinging" hammer blows.
Example: Dense, fine grain volcanic rock
Specimen breaks with some difficulty after several hammer blows.
Example: Vesicular, vugular, coarse-grained rock

A P P E N D I X   B 
Laboratory Tests 
Samples recovered from our field exploration performed for the Drilled Shaft 
Load Test Program for LT-B were visually examined and classified in our laboratory in 
Honolulu, Hawaii. Selected samples were tested to evaluate the physical characteristics 
and engineering properties of the soils encountered. The laboratory testing program 
was formulated with the emphasis on evaluating the moisture, density, plasticity, grain 
size, compressibility, and undrained shear strength of the soils encountered. 
The laboratory tests performed included determination of dry density and natural 
moisture content, Atterberg Limits, sieve analysis, consolidation, and triaxial 
compression tests. Results of the tests performed are presented on Plates B-1 through 
B-20. Detailed descriptions of the various tests performed for this exploration are 
presented below. 
Moisture content and dry density tests were performed on selected soil samples 
to aid in defining the interface between the various soil layers encountered. The 
moisture content tests were performed in general accordance with ASTM D 2216, and 
the dry unit weight (dry density) test was conducted in accordance with ASTM D 7263.  
The test results are presented on the Logs of Boring at the appropriate sample depths. 
Seven (7) Atterberg Limits tests were performed on selected soil samples to 
evaluate the liquid limit (LL), plastic limit (PL), and plasticity index (PI), as an aid in 
classifying the soils. The Atterberg Limits tests were performed in general accordance 
with ASTM D 4318. The test results are summarized on the Logs of Borings at the 
appropriate sample depths. Graphic presentation of the test results is provided on 
Plate B-1. 
To provide information on the particle size distribution of the soils encountered, 
two (2) Sieve Analysis tests were performed to evaluate the gradation characteristics 
and to aid in soil classification. The tests were performed in accordance with 
ASTM C 117 and C 136. Graphic presentation of the grain size distribution is provided 
on Plate B-2. 
Three (3) Consolidation tests were performed on selected soil samples to 
evaluate the compressibility characteristics of the on-site soils. These tests were 
performed in accordance with ASTM D 2435, and the test results are plotted on 
Plates B-3 through B-5. 
To evaluate the undrained shear strength of the clayey soils, fifteen (15) 
Unconsolidated Undrained Triaxial Compression tests in accordance with ASTM D 2850 
were performed on selected soil samples. The approximate in-situ effective overburden 
stress was used as the applied confining pressure for the “undisturbed” soil samples. 
The test results and the stress-strain curves are presented on Plates B-6 through B-20.

A P P E N D I X   C 
Load Test Shaft Installation 
The load test shaft was excavated using a Bauer BG-40 drill rig equipped with 
96-inch diameter digging bucket, core barrel, and clean-out bucket. Because artesian 
groundwater conditions and some soft soils were encountered during the drilling process, 
a permanent steel casing was installed to contain the groundwater in the load test shaft 
and to stabilize the soft soils during the drilling operations. We did not use synthetic slurry 
to stabilize the bored hole below the permanent steel casing during excavation for the load 
test shaft.  
The outside diameter (OD) of the permanent steel casing was 100 inches with a 
casing thickness of 0.75 inches. The bottom of the permanent casing extended to about 
54 feet below the ground surface and was embedded into a relatively stiff soil layer. A 
temporary steel casing (103 inches OD) was installed outside the permanent steel casing 
to help contain the artesian groundwater conditions and to serve as fall protection toward 
the open excavation. The temporary steel casing also was 0.75 inches thick. 
After excavation of Load Test Shaft LT-B to its specified tip elevation, the 
reinforcing steel cage was lowered into the drilled hole to the pre-determined elevation 
using two cranes. The reinforcing cage was spliced over the drilled hole utilizing 
mechanical couplers to connect the upper segment to the lower segment of the reinforcing 
cage. Reinforcing cage spacers were used to provide the clearance associated with a 
minimum concrete cover of 3 inches between the sidewalls of the drilled hole and the 
sides of the reinforcing cage. After the reinforcing cage was installed in the drilled hole, 
concrete was placed through a 5-inch ID steel tremie pipe starting from the bottom. 
During the load test shaft installation for LT-B, a Geolabs engineer observed the 
materials encountered during the shaft excavation, and a log of the subsurface materials 
encountered was maintained. In addition, the Geolabs engineer also maintained a log of 
the concrete volume placed in the load test shaft as a function of depth during concrete 
placement. The drilled shaft installation log maintained by the Geolabs engineer is 
presented as Plates C-1 through C-7. 
It should be noted that it was difficult to pump the concrete mix through the tremie 
pipe. During the initial placement of the concrete, the concrete pumping operation shook 
appreciably up until the fourth truck load of concrete. During placement of the fourth truck 
load of concrete (and subsequently), the pumping operation was much smoother. It was 
later discovered that the last segment of the tremie pipe disengaged at the bottom joint 
(32-foot segment), and concrete likely was discharging at the broken joint above the top of 
the concrete level instead of discharging at the bottom of the tremie pipe. Concrete 
placement for the load test shaft was halted temporarily at this point.  
Several attempts were made to retrieve the lower segment of the tremie pipe from 
within the load test shaft concrete without success. There was too much concrete placed 
LT-B  11/20/2009
Appendix C 
Load Test Shaft Installation 
W.O. 6000-40 
GEOLABS, INC. 
NOVEMBER 2009     Page C-2 
Hawaii ● California 
in the test shaft hole to remove the reinforcing cage and the concrete placed. Removal of 
the reinforcing cage likely would have damaged the instrumentation and load cell. 
Therefore, the decision was made to continue the concrete placement even with the 
potentially poor quality concrete in the load test shaft.  
The poor quality concrete in the load test shaft was later confirmed by the 
Crosshole Sonic Logging (CSL) tests and from the load testing. A replacement load test 
shaft was proposed to replace the LT-B with poor quality concrete; however, the 
replacement load test shaft for LT-B was not executed due to time constraints (expiring 
Right-of-Entry document) and other considerations.

A P P E N D I X   D 
Miniature Shaft Inspection Device 
To engage the end bearing component of the drilled shaft, we recommend using 
a miniature Shaft Inspection Device (Mini-SID) to check the base of the drilled shafts for 
cleanliness when using the end-bearing component for foundation support.  
The Mini-SID consists of a waterproof encased color video camera mounted in 
an inspection bell. The inspection bell is comprised of a weighted stainless steel skirt 
equipped with LED lights, two water jets, a compressed air inlet, two pass/fail indicators, 
and one depth of debris indicator. The two pass/fail indicators are marked at 0.5-inch 
and 1.5-inch. The depth of debris indicator is marked at 0.5-inch increments to a 
maximum of 3 inches. A three-hose umbilical cord used to transport air, water, electrical 
and video cables connect to the top of the inspection bell. 
The inspection bell is lowered to the bottom of the drilled shaft with the aid of an 
electrical hoist. Once on the bottom, live video is transmitted to a video control unit 
where the Engineer, Inspector, and Contractor can view and evaluate the shaft bottom 
at the same time. The live video can be recorded and stored to a DVD for later 
evaluation.  
The inspection is performed at five locations on the bottom of the shaft. The five 
locations include the center, north side, east side, south side, and west side of the shaft. 
The shaft is considered acceptable if 50% or more of the shaft bottom contains less 
than 0.5 inches of sediment. If any part of the shaft bottom contains more than 
1.5 inches of sediment, the shaft is considered unacceptable.  
The results of the Mini-SID performed on Load Test Shaft LT-B show that the 
average for all views of the shaft bottom containing less than 0.5 inches of sediment is 
40%, which is less than the typical 50% threshold. Several attempts were made to clean 
the bottom of the shaft; however, the nature of the bearing materials (cobbles and 
gravel in a fine grained soil matrix) made it very difficult to achieve that standard. Based 
on the Mini-SID inspections, the center and west views had more than 0.5 inches of 
sediments. However, none of the five views had an unacceptable amount of sediments 
(greater than 1.5 inches of sediments). 
After several attempts with only a small amount of sediments observed at the 
base of the load test shaft, we recommended proceeding with the reinforcing cage 
installation and concrete placement even with only an average of 40% of the shaft 
bottom with less than 0.5 inches of sediments because of the nature of the bearing 
materials. Results from the Mini-SID performed on Load Test Shaft LT-B are presented 
on Plates D-1 through D-6.

A P P E N D I X   E 
Concrete Tests 
Mass concrete is defined by the American Concrete Institute (ACI) as follows: 
Any volume of concrete with dimensions large enough to require that measures be 
taken to cope with generation of heat from hydration of the cement and attendant 
volume change to minimize cracking. As a general rule of thumb, any placement of 
structural concrete with a minimum dimension equal to or greater than 1 meter 
(39 inches) should be considered mass concrete. Similar consideration should be given 
to other concrete placements that do not meet this minimum dimension but contain 
ASTM C150 Type III or ASTM C1157 HE cement, accelerating admixtures, or 
cementitious materials in excess of 600 lbs/yd3 of concrete. 
All concrete generate heat as the cementitious materials hydrate. Heat is a 
byproduct of the hydration reactions, which gives concrete its strength and durability. In 
most placements, the heat escapes almost as rapidly as it is generated. In a mass 
concrete placement, the heat escapes more slowly than it is generated. The result is 
that temperatures within the concrete can get quite hot and high enough to damage the 
concrete. If the internal temperature exceeds 160 degrees Fahrenheit, the long term 
durability of some concretes may be affected by delayed ettringite formation (DEF). 
DEF is rare, and only certain concretes may be affected. When DEF occurs, the 
concrete paste expands and cracks the concrete with detrimental results, which may not 
be evident for many years. Additionally, while the interior of the concrete mass can be 
quite hot, the surface can be relatively cool. The resulting large temperature difference 
results in large thermal stresses, which can cause cracking of the surface. Historically, 
limiting the temperature difference between the interior and surface so that it is less 
than 35 degrees Fahrenheit has been found to prevent or minimize thermal cracking. 
Certain concretes are more tolerant of thermal cracking than others, and these 
concretes can withstand a higher temperature difference without thermally cracking. 
To prevent high internal temperatures and large temperature differences, an 
appropriate mix design should be selected. This will reduce other efforts to control 
temperatures and temperature differences after placement. The temperature rise of 
concrete is directly related to the types and quantities of cementitious materials in the 
concrete. An appropriate mix design contains the least amount of cementitious 
materials needed for strength and durability. Placeability of concrete also should factor 
into the concrete mix design. This sometimes increases the cementitious cement 
content. To reduce heat of hydration, Class F fly ash or slag cement typically is used to 
replace a portion of the cement. The percentage depends on several factors including 
environmental exposure and durability requirements. 
LT-B  11/20/2009
Appendix E 
Concrete Tests 
W.O. 6000-40 
GEOLABS, INC. 
NOVEMBER 2009     Page E-2 
Hawaii ● California 
Research suggests that the cracking of mass concrete may be lowered by a 
variety of methods, including the following: 
Reduction of the fresh concrete temperature 
Use of a larger maximum size aggregate 
Use of aggregate with a low coefficient of thermal expansion 
Use of crushed aggregate instead of smooth, round aggregate 
Replacement of cement with fly ash, slag, or suitable supplementary 
cementitious materials (SCMs) 
Entrained air 
Reduction of cement content and paste content 
To document the means and methods that are required and will be used, a 
thermal control plan will need to be developed for the project. A thermal control plan will 
allow all involved parties to agree on the measures that will be used, and the expected 
results. Such measures may include precooling of the concrete, cooling pipe installation 
and operation, insulation, temperature monitoring equipment and locations.  
For the load test shaft, some measures were taken in an attempt to reduce the 
heat generated in the drilled shaft concrete. However, it should be noted that we did not 
take extraordinary measures to keep the maximum concrete temperatures and the 
maximum temperature differentials to the specified levels for the project. In general, we 
used an air-entrained concrete to reduce the amount of cement in the concrete in an 
effort to reduce the overall heat generated from the hydrating concrete.  
The drilled shaft concrete mix utilized for the load test shaft was supplied by 
Island Ready Mix Concrete, Inc. (Mix No. 4569HVMS). The concrete temperature, 
slump, air content, and unit weight of the fresh concrete measured in the field during 
concrete placement are presented on Plate E-1. Results of the unconfined compression 
test results on the concrete cylinders at various cure days (age) are presented on 
Plate E-2. Results of the temperature measurements in the drilled shaft concrete at 
various depths for a period of about 9 days (216 hours) are presented on Plates E-3 
through E-15.  
Please note that some erratic readings were noted as evident in some of the 
temperature plots. The erratic readings in the temperature plots likely were due to water 
in the concrete migrating into the multiplexer housing through the strain gage and 
thermistor wires causing corrosion of the multiplexer connections. This was apparent 
after about 5 to 6 days of monitoring (please refer to Plates E-11 through E-15). The 
multiplexer connections were corroded beyond repair.  
Please also disregard the temperature plot on Plate E-5 (Level 3 at 101.5-foot 
level) because this was apparently the area containing the poor quality concrete from 
the detached tremie pipe.

A P P E N D I X   F 
Crosshole Sonic Logging 
CSL testing was performed to evaluate the integrity of the concrete drilled shaft 
for the portion of the load test shaft above the load cell only. To ensure that a fracture 
plane is created at the base of the load cell, steel CSL tubes were not extended below 
the top of the load cell plate. 
Generally, CSL tests measure the travel time of an ultrasonic pulse wave to 
travel from a signal source in one access tube to a receiver in another access tube. The 
test is performed by lowering the signal and receiver probe to the bottom of 
two separate access tubes. The transmitter and receiver probes are then pulled upward 
simultaneously so they are always located at the same height. While being pulled, the 
probes are continuously sending and receiving ultrasonic pulse waves. The first arrival 
time of the ultrasonic pulse wave is recorded every 2 inches (50 mm). 
The CSL test was conducted using the CHUM CSL Testing equipment 
manufactured by Piletest. CSL tests were performed between all the possible access 
tube combinations in the load test shaft (8 tubes in the shaft resulting in 28 profiles per 
shaft). The northern most access tube was designated as Tube 1. 
Steel access tubes were installed in the load test shaft above the load cell top 
plate only. Generally, the steel access tubes were extended approximately 7 feet above 
the top of the shaft concrete for access considerations. Please note that the top of 
concrete is approximately 5 feet below the ground surface. Potable water was used to 
fill the access tubes following concrete placement, and the Contractor monitored the 
water level in the access tubes to keep the water level in the access tubes until the CSL 
tests. 
As a general guideline, increases in FATs of 10% or less may be considered 
insignificant. Increases in FATs between 10% and 25% usually are considered minor, 
but verified defects have been discovered in some cases, while increases in FATs 
greater than 25% usually indicate anomalies worthy of consideration and further 
evaluation. Changes in relative energy can sometimes indicate defects depending on 
the degree of the reduction and the associated increase in FAT. 
The average apparent ultrasonic pulse wave velocity can be determined from the 
CSL test results by dividing the average tube distances by the average first arrival times 
for each tube combination in a shaft. It should be noted that the average apparent 
ultrasonic pulse wave velocity computed in this manner is not an absolute number and 
should be used with caution because the actual tube distances below the top of the shaft 
are not known accurately. 
Based on the above discussion and our field observations, we believe Load Test 
Shaft LT-B had some significant anomalies in the shaft concrete. The anomalies noted 
LT-B  11/20/2009
Appendix F 
Crosshole Sonic Logging 
W.O. 6000-40 
GEOLABS, INC. 
NOVEMBER 2009     Page F-2 
Hawaii ● California 
also were confirmed by field observations during the concrete placement. The anomalies 
also prevented proper interpretation of the load test results as some crushing of the 
concrete was observed above the load cell top bearing plate. Based on the CSL test 
results on Plates F-3.1 through F-3.14, we recommend further investigation into the 
concrete quality in Load Test Shaft LT-B due to suspected poor quality concrete in the 
load test shaft above the load cell top bearing plate.

The apparent sonic wave velocity is an indication of the overall quality of the concrete and was estimated by 
dividing the average tube distance by the average first arrival time for all tube combinations.  Sonic wave 
velocities for cast-in-place concrete generally range from 10,000 to 13,000 feet per second.
The tested length represents the average length of tube accessible during CSL logging including the stickup 
above the concrete.

A P P E N D I X   G 
Osterberg Load Test 
An Osterberg load cell test was conducted on the load test shaft (designated as 
LT-B) by LOADTEST, Inc. on September 15, 2009. The load test shaft was excavated 
by CMZ of Hawaii, Inc. under the observation of representatives from Geolabs, Inc. The 
load cell assembly and test shaft instrumentation were carried out under the direction of 
Mr. Denton A. Kort of LOADTEST, Inc. 
Detailed discussions, data, and analysis of the O-cell load test are presented in 
the report entitled “Report on Drilled Shaft Load Testing (Osterberg Method), LT-B, 
Honolulu HCTC, Waipahu, Hawaii (LT-9581-2)” dated September 24, 2009 prepared by 
LOADTEST, Inc. A copy of the report is included in this appendix for ease of reference.

EXECUTIVE SUMMARY 
On September 15, 2009, we tested a nominal 96-inch (2,438-mm) diameter 
dedicated test shaft constructed by CMZ of Hawaii  Mr. Denton A. Kort of 
LOADTEST, Inc. carried out the test.  CMZ of Hawaii completed construction of the 
125.2-foot (38.16-meter) deep shaft under natural groundwater seepage on 
September 2, 2009.  Sub-surface conditions at the test shaft location consist 
primarily of silt and clay.  Representatives of Geolabs, Inc. and others observed 
construction and testing of the shaft. 
The maximum sustained bi-directional load applied to the shaft was 2,886 kips 
(12.84 MN).  At the maximum load, the displacements above and below the O-cell 
were 0.795 inches (20.19 mm) and 0.109 inches (2.78 mm), respectively.  Unit shear 
data calculated from strain gages indicated a maximum net unit side shear of 9.0 ksf 
(432 kPa) between strain gage level 2 and strain gage level 1.   
It should be noted that during the test, significant deformations of the upper bearing 
plate were observed, and excessive crushing was measured in the top plate telltale 
measurements.  Extremely high, non-uniform strain readings from the first two levels 
of strain gages above the O-cell also suggest a very low-modulus concrete above 
the O-cell.  This may result from a problem during construction, wherein an unknown 
amount of concrete was deposited through water due to a broken tremie pipe. 
A sonar caliper (SONICALIPER) was used to generate profiles of the shaft 
excavation sidewalls prior to installation of the O-cell assembly.  A summary of the 
caliper data and the dimensions used in the analyses are included in Appendix E. 
LIMITATIONS OF EXECUTIVE SUMMARY 
We include this executive summary to provide a very brief presentation of some of 
the key elements of this O-cell test.  It is by no means intended to be a 
comprehensive or stand-alone representation of the test results.  The full text of the 
report and the attached appendices contain important information which the 
engineer can use to come to more informed conclusions about the data presented 
herein.

SITE CONDITIONS AND SHAFT CONSTRUCTION 
Site Sub-surface Conditions:  The sub-surface stratigraphy at the general location 
of the test shaft is reported to consist of silt and clay.  The generalized subsurface 
profile is included in Figure A and a boring log indicating conditions near the shaft is 
presented in Appendix C. 
Test Shaft Construction:  CMZ of Hawaii began excavation of the dedicated test 
shaft on August 25, 2009 and performed the final cleanout and concreting on 
September 2, 2009.  We understand that the nominal 96-inch (2,438-mm) diameter 
test shaft was excavated to a tip elevation of -116.2 feet (-35.42 meters) under 
natural groundwater seepage.  The shaft was started by installing a 98.5-inch 
(2,502-mm) O.D. casing to a tip elevation of -45.1 feet (-13.75 meters) using a rig-
mounted tool.  A digging bucket was used for excavating the shaft.  After cleaning 
the base with a cleanout bucket on September 1, 2009, the SONICALIPER was 
used to generate a profile of the shaft excavation.  The lower reinforcing cage was 
then lowered into the excavation and temporarily supported from the casing and left 
in the excavation overnight.  On the morning of September 2, 2009, the cage was 
removed and the base of the shaft cleaned with a cleanout bucket.  The base of the 
shaft was then inspected using a Mini-SID shaft inspection device.  After the base 
inspection, the lower cage was lowered into the excavation, the upper cage spliced 
to the lower and the entire assembly suspended from the casing.  Concrete was 
then delivered by pump into the base of the shaft through a 5-inch (125-mm) 
diameter pipe.  At some point during the concrete pour, the first section of tremie 
pipe became disengaged from the other sections above it.  The break in the pipe 
was not discovered until an unknown quantity of concrete was discharged through 
the water on top of the O-cell assembly.  The contractor was unable to retrieve the 
32-foot (9.75-meter) section of tremie pipe, which was left, presumably resting on 
the tip of the excavation, extending through the O-cell assembly.  The remainder of 
the concrete was delivered using the same procedure as above, however we 
understand that the contractor was not able to maintain any concrete embedment of 
the tremie pipe for the remainder of the pour.  The final concrete elevation was +4.1 
feet (+1.25 meters).  Representatives of Geolabs, Inc. and others observed 
construction of the shaft.  
OSTERBERG CELL TESTING 
Shaft Instrumentation:  Test shaft instrumentation and assembly was carried out 
under the direction of Denton Kort of LOADTEST, Inc. between August 27, 2009 and 
September 1, 2009.  The loading assembly consisted of one 34-inch (870-mm) 
diameter O-cell located 15.7 feet (4.78 meters) above the tip of shaft.  Calibrations of 
the O-cell and instrumentation used for this test are included in Appendix B.  
LT-B  11/20/2009
LT-B - Honolulu HCTC 
Page 3 
Waipahu, HI (LT-9581-2) 
DEEP FOUNDATION TESTING, EQUIPMENT & SERVICES • SPECIALIZING IN OSTERBERG CELL (O-cell®) TECHNOLOGY 
O-cell® is a registered trademark. 
O-cell testing instrumentation included four Linear Vibrating Wire Displacement 
Transducers (LVWDTs - Geokon Model 4450 series) positioned between the lower 
and upper plates of the O-cell assembly to measure expansion (two adjacent to the 
O-cell and two near the perimeter of the bearing plate).  Two telltale casings 
(nominal ½-inch steel pipe) were attached to the reinforcing cage, diametrically 
opposed, extending from the tip of the rebar cage to beyond the top of concrete to 
measure tip displacement.   
Two telltale casings (nominal ½-inch steel pipe) were also attached to the reinforcing 
cage, diametrically opposed, extending from the top of the O-cell assembly to 
beyond the top of concrete to measure compression above the O-cell.   
Strain gages were used to assess the side shear load transfer of the shaft above 
and below the Osterberg cell assembly.  Six levels of three and 5 levels of two 
diametrically-opposed vibrating wire embedment strain gages (Geokon Model 4200 
series) were installed in the shaft above the base of the O-cell assembly and two 
levels of three were installed in the shaft below it.  Details concerning the 
instrumentation position appear in Table B and Figures A & B.  Data collected from 
these instruments are included in Appendix A. 
Test Arrangement:  Throughout the load test, key elements of shaft response were 
monitored using the equipment and instruments described herein.  Telltales were 
installed in the nominal ½-inch steel pipes (described under Shaft Instrumentation) 
and monitored by Linear Variable Displacement Transducers (LVDTs - RDP series).  
Two automated digital survey levels (Leica NA 3000 Series) were used to monitor 
the top of shaft movement from a distance of approximately 36 feet (10.9 meters). 
Data collected from these instruments are included in Appendix A, Page 1.   
A Bourdon pressure gage and two electronic pressure transducers were used to 
measure the pressure applied to the O-cell at each load interval.  We used the 
transducers for automatically setting and maintaining loads and for data analysis and 
real time plotting.  The Bourdon gage was used as a check on the transducers.  
There was close agreement between the Bourdon gage and the pressure 
transducers.   
Data Acquisition:  All instrumentation were connected through a data logger 
(Campbell Scientific CR-10) to a laptop computer allowing data to be recorded and 
stored automatically at 120-second intervals and displayed in real time.  The same 
laptop computer synchronized to the data logging system was used to acquire the 
Leica NA3000 data sets.  
Testing Procedures:  As with all of our tests, we begin by pressurizing the O-cell in 
order to break the tack welds that hold it closed (for handling and for placement in 
the shaft) and to form the fracture plane in the concrete surrounding the base of the 
O-cell.  After the break occurs, we immediately release the pressure and then begin 
the loading procedure.  Zero readings for all instrumentation are taken prior to the 
LT-B  11/20/2009
LT-B - Honolulu HCTC 
Page 4 
Waipahu, HI (LT-9581-2) 
DEEP FOUNDATION TESTING, EQUIPMENT & SERVICES • SPECIALIZING IN OSTERBERG CELL (O-cell®) TECHNOLOGY 
O-cell® is a registered trademark. 
preliminary weld-breaking load-unload cycle, which in this case involved a maximum 
applied load of 607 kips (2.7 MN).  
The Osterberg cell load test was conducted as follows: We pressurized the 34-inch 
(870-mm) diameter O-cell, with its base located 15.7 feet (4.78 meters) above the 
base of shaft to assess the combined end bearing and lower side shear below the 
O-cell and the upper side shear above.  We loaded the shaft in 16 loading 
increments to a bi-directional gross O-cell load of 2,886 kips (12.84 MN).  An 
additional loading increment at 1L-17 was attempted, but the O-cell exceeded its 
maximum stroke and was rapidly depressurized and the test was concluded. 
We carried out the test using Procedure A: Quick Test loading schedule (ASTM 
D1143-07 Standard Test Methods for Deep Foundations Under Static Axial 
Compressive Load), holding each successive load increment constant for eight 
minutes (Appendix A, Figure 2).  The data logger automatically recorded the 
instrument readings every 120 seconds, but herein we report only the 2, 4 and 8-
minute readings (where applicable) during each increment of maintained load.  
Additional readings are reported during increments 1L-6, 1L-8, 1L-13 and 1U-1.    
TEST RESULTS AND ANALYSES 
General:  The loads applied by the O-cell act in two opposing directions, resisted by 
the capacity of the shaft above and below.  Theoretically, the O-cell does not impose 
an additional upward load until its expansion force exceeds the buoyant weight of 
the shaft above the O-cell.  Therefore, net load, which is defined as gross O-cell load 
minus the buoyant weight of the shaft above, is used to determine side shear 
resistance above the O-cell and to construct the equivalent top-loaded load-
settlement curve.  For this test we calculated a buoyant weight of shaft of 539 kips 
(2.40 MN) above the O-cell.   
For the purposes of analyses herein, we use the maximum sustained loading at 1L-
16 of 2,886 kips (12.84 MN).  The maximum applied load of 3,006 kips (13.4 MN) 
occurred at the 0-minute reading of increment 1L-17, at which point the 
displacements above and below the O-cell were 0.772 inches (19.60 mm) and 0.132 
inches (3.34 mm), respectively. 
It should be noted that during the test, significant deformations of the upper bearing 
plate were observed, and excessive crushing was measured in the top plate telltale 
measurements.  Extremely high, non-uniform strain readings from the first two levels 
of strain gages above the O-cell also suggest a very low-modulus concrete above 
the O-cell.  This may result from a problem during construction, wherein an unknown 
amount of concrete was deposited through water due to a broken tremie pipe.  As a 
result, the Engineer should exercise caution when using the values reported herein 
LT-B  11/20/2009
LT-B - Honolulu HCTC 
Page 5 
Waipahu, HI (LT-9581-2) 
DEEP FOUNDATION TESTING, EQUIPMENT & SERVICES • SPECIALIZING IN OSTERBERG CELL (O-cell®) TECHNOLOGY 
O-cell® is a registered trademark. 
as the upward displacements at a minimum are not representative of uniform shaft 
deformation.   
Pile Shape and Dimensions:  SONICALIPER profiles were taken of the 
excavation to determine the geometry of the shaft.  In the analyses, we use a 
combination of the nominal shaft dimensions and those obtained from the sonar 
caliper profile between elevations +2.9 feet and -111.1 feet.  A summary of the sonar 
caliper data and the dimensions used in the analyses are included in Appendix D.  
The Engineer should consider the effect of shaft shape on shear resistance when 
evaluating the test results. 
Upper Side Shear Resistance:  The maximum upward applied net load to the 
upper side shear was 2,348 kips (10.44 MN) which occurred at load interval 1L-16.  
At this loading, the upward movement of the O-cell top plate was 0.795 inches 
(20.19 mm) (Appendix A, Page 3, Figures 1 - 3).  Note that this measurement 
includes excessive shaft compression above the top plate which may be due to 
crushing of low-modulus concrete.  
In order to assess the side shear resistance of the test shaft, loads are calculated 
based on the strain gage data (Appendix A, Pages 4 through 9, Figures 4 and 5) and 
an estimates of shaft stiffness (AE) which are presented below.  We used the ACI 
formula (Ec=57000√f´c) to calculate an elastic modulus for the concrete, where f’c 
was reported to be 6,062 psi (41.80 MPa) on the day of the test.  This, combined 
with the area of reinforcing steel and measured shaft diameter, provided an average 
shaft stiffness (AE) of 40,200,000 kips (179,000 MN) above the O-cell in the cased 
zone, 35,000,000 kips (156,000 MN) above the O-cell in the uncased zone and 
38,000,000 kips (169,000 MN) below the O-cell.  Mobilized net unit shear curves are 
presented in Figure 6.  Net unit shear values for loading increment 1L-16 follow in

Combined End Bearing and Lower Side Shear Resistance:  The maximum O-cell 
load applied to the combined end bearing and lower side shear was 2,886 kips 
(12.84 MN) which occurred at load interval 1L-16 (Appendix A, Page 3, Figures 1 - 
3).  At this loading, the average downward movement of the O-cell base was 0.109 
inches (2.78 mm).  Note that this downward displacement is estimated, based on tip 
displacement readings adjusted for theoretical compression between the O-cell and 
the tip of the shaft.  The load distribution shown in Figure 5 indicates that the entire 
downward applied load was taken in side shear below the O-cell and no load was 
transferred to end bearing at the maximum measured tip displacement. 
Shaft Compression Comparison:  The measured maximum shaft compression, 
averaged from two telltales, is 0.771 inches (19.58 mm) at 1L-16 (Appendix A, Page 
1).  Using a weighted average shaft stiffness of 37,450,000 kips (166,580 MN) and 
the load distribution in Figure 5 at 1L-16, we calculated an elastic compression of 
0.017 inches (0.44 mm) over the length of the compression telltales.  We believe 
that the difference in the measured and calculated values is due to crushing of low 
modulus concrete above the top plate of the O-cell assembly.

APPENDIX  D
LT-B  11/20/2009
The enclosed report contains the data and analysis summary for the SoniCaliper
shaft caliper, performed at HCTC Preliminary Tests (LT-B), Waipahu, HI on
Tuesday, September 01, 2009 by Denny Kort.  The shaft was calipered from a
reference depth of 12.0 feet to a maximum depth of 126.0 feet.
The minimum concrete volume is calculated to be 237.1 yd³, based on the area of
the calipered cross-sections and a Top of Concrete depth of 11.0 feet.  (Note that
this includes theoretical volume based on a nominal shaft cross-sectional area
between depths of 126.0 feet and 131.0 feet, which was not calipered.)
Note that significant wall encroachment (greater than 6.0 inches) was calculated at
the depths listed below:

SPECIALIZING IN OSTERBERG CELL (O-cell®) TECHNOLOGY 
O-cell® and SoniCaliper® are registered trademarks. 
INTERPRETATION OF SONICALIPER FIELD DATA REPORT 
General: T he SoniCaliper is a profiling sonar 
device, specially adapt ed to functi on in drillin g 
fluids.  Each 360° pass generated
 with the SoniCaliper d evice produces up to o ne hundred  
twenty individual echo r eturns (profile data poin ts).  In the attached figures (profile ring plots),  
the diamond points rep resent individual profile  data points.  A circle is fitted to the  data points 
using a proprietary double-step lea
st-squares t echnique, t o approximate the cross-sect ional 
geometry of the shaft.  A center location and diameter are calculated for each profile ring circle. 
Deployment:  The  device is lowere d into the shaft excavation in  incremental depths.  At  each 
depth, a 360° sweep of the shaft wall is performed.  The device is assumed to hang vertically in 
the shaft (any deviation from verticality can be noted using onboard pitch and roll sensors).  Any 
twist in  the  device rela tive to its initial or
ientation is co mpensated by an onbo ard compass 
sensor. 
Calibration:  Because  the properties of drilling fluids v
ary widely,  a calibrat ion must be  
performed for each shaft to determine fluid wavespeed (an d thus calculate range data).  This is 
done by sel ecting a profile ring of known diameter (usua
lly, but not always the upper-most 
profile ring)  as the “calibration ring”.  The data analysis then ba
ck-calculates the fluid
wavespeed based on  the known d iameter of this ring.   The  fluid wavespeed is assumed to be 
constant over the entire column of 
fluid depth. 
Shaft Verticality:  T
o determine  
shaft verticality, a profile rin
g 
(usually, but not always the
calibration r ing) is se lected as the  
“datum ring”.  The ge ometric centers 
of the datum ring and all other profile 
rings are compared.  The “center
offset” listed on the f igures indicates 
the divergence of each profile rin
g 
center poin t from the datum ring 
center poin t.  “Encroachment” is 
presented graphically as the shaded  
area representing the p ortion of th e 
shaft wall which would encroach into 
the perfectly vertical projection of the
